$(document).ready(function(){
															var myPlayer = $("#jquery_jplayer_0"),
																myPlayerData,
																options = {
																	ready: function (event) {
																		if(event.jPlayer.status.noVolume) {
																			$(".jp-gui").addClass("jp-no-volume");
																		}
																		$(this).jPlayer("setMedia", {
																			mp3: "http://www.radioram.pl/img/articles/31630/To-on-wynalazl-szczepionke-przeciwko-tyfusowi-Powstanie-biografia-slynnego-lwowskiego-biologa-4-53854.mp3"
																		});
																	},
																	timeupdate: function(event) {
																		myControl.progress.slider("value", event.jPlayer.status.currentPercentAbsolute);
																	},
																	swfPath: "/js/jplayer",
																	supplied: "mp3",
																	cssSelectorAncestor: "#jp_container_0",
																	wmode: "window",
																	keyEnabled: true
																},
																myControl = {
																	progress: $(options.cssSelectorAncestor + " .jp-progress-slider"),
																};

																myPlayer.jPlayer(options);

																myPlayerData = myPlayer.data("jPlayer");

																var wwidth = $(options.cssSelectorAncestor).closest("div.jqplayer").width();
																														
																$("div.jplayer_player").width(wwidth);
																$("div.jp-gui").width(wwidth-12);
																var pwidth = wwidth;
																$(options.cssSelectorAncestor + " .jp-progress-slider").width(pwidth - 100);
																																														
																$(".jp-gui ul li").hover(
																	function() { $(this).addClass("ui-state-hover"); },
																	function() { $(this).removeClass("ui-state-hover"); }
																);

																myControl.progress.slider({
																	animate: "fast",
																	max: 100,
																	range: "min",
																	step: 0.1,
																	value : 0,
																	slide: function(event, ui) {
																		var sp = myPlayerData.status.seekPercent;
																		if(sp > 0) {
																			myPlayer.jPlayer("playHead", ui.value * (100 / sp));
																		} else {
																			setTimeout(function() {
																				myControl.progress.slider("value", 0);
																			}, 0);
																		}
																	}
																});

																$( window ).resize(function() {
																	var wwidth = $("#contentContainer #content .mainArticleContainer .articleContent").width();
																	$("div.jplayer_player").width(wwidth);

Le Guin, the immensely popular author who brought literary depth and a tough-minded feminist sensibility to science fiction and fantasy with books like â€œThe Left Hand of Darknessâ€ and the Earthsea series, died on Monday at her home in Portland, Ore. She was 88.Her son, Theo Downes-Le Guin, confirmed the death. He did not specify a cause but said she had been in poor health for several months.Ms. Le Guin embraced the standard themes of her chosen genres: sorcery and dragons, spaceships and planetary conflict. But even when her protagonists are male, they avoid the macho posturing of so many science fiction and fantasy heroes. The conflicts they face are typically rooted in a clash of cultures and resolved more by conciliation and self-sacrifice than by swordplay or space battles.Her books have been translated into more than 40 languages and have sold millions of copies worldwide. Several, including â€œThe Left Hand of Darknessâ€ â€” set on a planet where the customary gender distinctions do not apply â€” have been in print for almost 50 years. The critic Harold Bloom lauded Ms. Le Guin as â€œa superbly imaginative creator and major stylistâ€ who â€œhas raised fantasy into high literature for our time.â€In addition to more than 20 novels, she was the author of a dozen books of poetry, more than 100 short stories (collected in multiple volumes), seven collections of essays, 13 books for children and five volumes of translation, including the Tao Te Ching of Lao Tzu and selected poems by the Chilean Nobel Prize winner Gabriela Mistral. She also wrote a guide for writers.Ms. Le Guinâ€™s fictions range from young-adult adventures to wry philosophical fables. They combine compelling stories, rigorous narrative logic and a lean but lyrical style to draw readers into what she called the â€œinner landsâ€ of the imagination. Such writing, she believed, could be a moral force.â€œIf you cannot or will not imagine the results of your actions, thereâ€™s no way you can act morally or responsibly,â€ she told The Guardian in an interview in 2005. â€œLittle kids canâ€™t do it; babies are morally monsters â€” completely greedy. Their imagination has to be trained into foresight and empathy.â€The writerâ€™s â€œpleasant duty,â€ she said, is to ply the readerâ€™s imagination with â€œthe best and purest nourishment that it can absorb.â€She was born Ursula Kroeber in Berkeley, Calif., on Oct. 21, 1929, the youngest of four children and the only daughter of two anthropologists, Alfred L. Kroeber and Theodora Kracaw Kroeber. Her father was an expert on the Native Americans of California, and her mother wrote an acclaimed book, â€œIshi in Two Worldsâ€ (1960), about the life and death of Californiaâ€™s â€œlast wild Indian.â€At a young age, Ms. Le Guin immersed herself in books about mythology, among them James Frazerâ€™s â€œThe Golden Bough,â€ classic fantasies like Lord Dunsanyâ€™s â€œA Dreamerâ€™s Tales,â€ and the science-fiction magazines of the day. But in early adolescence she lost interest in science fiction, because, she recalled, the stories â€œseemed to be all about hardware and soldiers: White men go forth and conquer the universe.â€She graduated from Radcliffe College in 1951, earned a masterâ€™s degree in romance literature of the Middle Ages and Renaissance from Columbia University in 1952, and won a Fulbright fellowship to study in Paris. There she met and married another Fulbright scholar, Charles Le Guin, who survives her.On their return to the United States, she abandoned her graduate studies to raise a family; the Le Guins eventually settled in Portland, where Mr. Le Guin taught history at Portland State University.Besides her husband and son, Ms. Le Guin is survived by two daughters, Caroline and Elisabeth Le Guin; two brothers, Theodore and Clifton Kroeber; and four grandchildren.By the early 1960s Ms. Le Guin had written five unpublished novels, mostly set in an imaginary Central European country called Orsinia. Eager to find a more welcoming market, she decided to try her hand at genre fiction.Her first science-fiction novel, â€œRocannonâ€™s World,â€ came out in 1966. Two years later she published â€œA Wizard of Earthsea,â€ the first in a series about a made-up world where the practice of magic is as precise as any science, and as morally ambiguous.The first three Earthsea books â€” the other two were â€œThe Tombs of Atuanâ€ (1971) and â€œThe Farthest Shoreâ€ (1972) â€” were written, at the request of her publisher, for young adults.
